And immediately pause your left hand near the patient's left palpebra if the eye in which the water is flowing is the left eye; or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
And immediately with your right hand take an almond if the eye is left; or with your left hand if it is right.
And immediately pause the extremity of the almond near the crown by the thickness of the ray in the album of the eye from the smaller lacrimal part, and immediately squeeze the almond with virtue and rotate your hand with it.
And immediately penetrate into the album of the eye and sense that the almond has already reached the desired point because of the vision.
And it is necessary that the path of the almond to the prominence be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the darkness, and it is the crown of the eye; for you will see with that pupil through the vision of the eye by the clarity of the tunic cornea.
And immediately pause the almond in the place where the water is, and immediately attract it downwards once or twice more, and thus if the water descends soon, then look at the patient over which their vision is soon to be clouded, and the almond in its own eye.
And immediately pause for a little while; thus if the water has descended, then you will lay that other way before the almond is dropped.
And thus when it is dried and does not descend, then you will extract the almond with ease, and rotate it with your hand little by little, and immediately dissolve in the water a small amount of clear gemstone salt and wash the eye outward; and immediately pause outside the eye with a cotton or stopper or lanolin infused in rose oil and albumen of egg whites, and bind with water the patient's eye.
But we have those of our time who pause on that which is mixed with albumen of egg whites.
And if they do not obey your almond at the instrument of the eye by its hardness - for it is some of those men whose eyes are very hard - then you must take the spatula which is called the "alberid" and this is its form:
And thus perforate with it that conjunction, and do not stop your hand in the hole, and it is only if you make a way for the almond to reach the subtle location.
And immediately insert the almond according to what we have said.
And you must complete your work, be prepared for the patient's sleep, which is made to descend in a place of darkness, and it is forbidden from all movements.
And pause in the nature of that water so that its nature is aligned, and do not trouble their head to the right or left side at all.
And be the ligature according to its disposition within three days.
And immediately loosen that in the darkest and most obscure manner, and make them see some causes, and then return to the ligature within seven days.
And do not need to do that in the hour of curations or soon after the infusion. They had need that you should alienate this because the water flows more lightly by great and vehement regard.
But if an apostema occurs, then you must loosen the eye within seven days and correct it with water that allays the apostema.
Then you must absolve the vision from that and pause on the face of the mouth for which the direction of the left eye is regulated for a few days, existing in the darkest.
And immediately be guided by that greatest degree and converted into its own causes.
And know that in some cases the disciple is not exempt from seeing some things, and immediately you will be bold on the operation.
And it has been reached to us of some of those who are at the Artach, and some said that a fire is made in the Ayrac, great and perforated, whereupon the water is drained.
But meanwhile I do not see anything like it in our land which has been perfected, nor is it written in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the species of magnesia for which there is something above those which are ignorant of those:
And they are made of iron or of copper properly, and they are the extremities of theirs with this subtlety triangular, the pointed end just as that is the form.
Twenty-Fourth Chapter.
On the Cure of Carnousness in the Nose.
When in the nose there are born various fleshy growths adjusted of which some cause is assembled to scorpion's claw or polyp's foot; and this illness is called Polypus because it is like that of a fish represented. And from it there comes cancerous flesh, blackened and dark in color; and from it there comes lion-like flesh not of dark color.
Therefore in that which is in these lion-like flesh not fraudulent nor cancerous, it is necessary to make the patient sit between your hands and place them in the sun, and anoint the nose with oil, and pierce the tongue once in those flesh. And immediately extract that from the parts outside, and immediately incise there what you have comprehended of those with a subtle spatula from one part into which you know that all the flesh is removed; for if any cause remains from it which abscission is possible, then you must ratify that with one of the subtle instruments with ease until no cause remains from it. But if you notice blood or an apostema occurs, then immediately pause on that which is necessary; for it is one of the malignant apostemas, then you must cut and cauterize that until the blood is absorbed and all the cavities are removed.
And immediately be guided in the nose with the incision, whether it be with water or oil. But if the nose is open and there is humidity proceeding into the palate, then you will know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you will know that behind it is carnousness in the pustules or bones of the nose to which no abscission has been achieved.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the thread of faith into one who has some thickness, and nourish him in his very marrow, and pause between each nourishment the quantity of said or less, and enervate the illness and insert one extremity of the thread in the nose with the ray or another cause which is possible to it.
And since this will have been done, then draw their odorment into the channels of the nose, and be exhausted above their goiter, for many times they seem like children in schools, and it is a light cause to that which they wish to do.
And immediately pause the two extremities of the thread, one extremity that comes out on the nose and the other that remains in the nose, and immediately administer a serration of flesh to the knots that are in the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is already united with the knots of the thread.
And immediately extract the thread, and pause in the nose about the abstraction of blood one quart, and do this for three days or more until the flesh is completely consumed that remains of the flesh.
And immediately in the end of the cause, pause in the nose a lead cannula for several days until it is healed.
But if you degenerate from cure that exists, use this one.
And this is the form of the instrument, the capillary purge maker, with which oils and medicines are distilled in the nose:
And make this of silver or copper, which is like a small open lamp, just as a small shell, and the mouth similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a reed, and the cannula of the instrument for capillary purging should be flat, open; to which there is a handle at the end, with which you can grasp that when it is filled with oil or water that you want from the juices and suitable causes.
Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
On the Holes in the Extremities of the Nose.
Many times it is born a hole in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in several days until it is seen as the regard of it.
And therefore it is necessary that you incise that in the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all that. And immediately pause on the spot or cauterize or medicine which is competent for that place.
But if it is delayed in its incision until it is magnified, then consider whether it is hardened, blackened, and lacks feeling; then do not touch that with iron because it is a cancerous apostema; for many times those who incise this apostema are driven by great malice towards him whom it had previously.
And if the apostema is of lion-like touch and not of dark color, and times that incision is possible in all that, then evacuate the patient and incise that without fear, and cure the place with what exists and constrain it until it is healed.
Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
On the Suture of the Nose and Lips and Earlobe when continuity is dissolved by wound or appearance.
Know that when there occurs dissolution of continuity in one of these cartilages, few confer on that operation except in some men.
Therefore, when you are driven by some cause of those, know that if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, then immediately adjust the two lips of the wound with suture, and immediately cure that until it is healed.
And if the lips of dissolution of continuity are already separated, and each is made whole, then it is necessary that you excise each part of its apparent cord until blood comes out. And immediately adjust the two lips with suture and strengthen those, and on top of those powder with dragon's blood and threefold thrice, and pause on the powder a plaster of diaphonic emplastrum, or of some other consolidative emplastrum, and let that be bound for two days or three, and immediately release that and replace it with another plaster, and let that be until the threads are absorbed, and immediately cure with anointing until it is healed.
But the method of suture is that you add the solution of continuity with threads, just as we have recounted in the suture of the belly, or add the solution of continuity with thread, just as I and thou wouldst make it known.
Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
On the Extraction of those causes that come into the two lips in the parts behind.
Many times it happens to many men in the parts behind their lips that some hard and rough apostemas appear, resembling errors, and some are smaller and some are greater.
Therefore, it is necessary that you convert the lips and fissures of each of those hardenings throughout all parts, and immediately fill the wound with sagitarius triticum until the blood is restrained, and immediately be washed with hot oil and be cured the place with what exists, until it is healed.
Twenty-Eighth Chapter.
On the Incision of the flesh adapted to the Gums.
Great many times there is a swelling over the gummed flesh which the ancients called "albulus", therefore it is necessary that you suspend that with once or what you have with the forfices and incise that as it should be, and let the pus flow or the blood.
And immediately pause on the place with sagitarius triticum or other powders that are stimulating or exciting.
And if that flesh returns, then immediately incise it secondarily, and cauterize it, for the cauterization will not return again.
Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Reason of Teeth with Iron.
When in the surface of the teeth of the parts behind and those of the forehead, and between the gums are adjusted rough, sharp, and blackened ones, some times they are blackened and are made citrines or greenish until the gum is corrupted and the teeth become loose.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and pause the head in your seat, and ratify the teeth in which those roughs and causes appear like sand until there remains no cause from them, and this makes of the blackness and citrinity and of the others until they are purified.
Therefore, if this is what is in the beginning of the reason, well it is; and otherwise, then you must iterate the reason over those for another day and secondly and thirdly until it comes to the final statement as you wish.
And know that teeth have need of many remedies of diverse forms and figures according to how it is prepared for your operation. And therefore since the remedies for the tooth that is extracted from behind are different from those used between teeth according to other forms, and these forms of remedies must be prepared by you which you will see soon:
Thirtieth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
It is necessary that you cure the tooth of its pain with all diligence and patience throughout its entire extraction, for there is no cause that supplies the vigor of it when it is extracted, since it is a subtle substance within which there is an excuse in the extraction of it.
But it is necessary when the patient is prepared to extract that tooth, that you be firmly certain which tooth is ailing, for many times the pain deceives the patient and makes him think it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and immediately the pain does not cease until the sick and aching tooth is extracted, as we have already seen by the operation of the barber's.
Therefore, since you must be certain which tooth is ailing, and you know well, it is necessary that you scarify in the circuit of the tooth with spatula in the part where there is some virtue, until the tooth is dissolved from the gum of all parts, and immediately proceed to cauterize that with your own fingers or with fine forceps, little by little, until it becomes concave.
And immediately afterwards, hold the tooth firmly with great tenacity for good articulation or strong extraction, and pause the head of the patient between your fingers that well keep it so as not to trouble him, and immediately remove the tooth according to rectitude in manner so as not to break it, nor give greater pain to the patient.
And therefore if it is not forgotten, then immediately take one of those instruments and place it at a distance from the tooth on all sides with ease and effort to remove it in this way as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is bruised or corroded, then it is necessary that you wrap with cloth the bruise and strengthen it at the end of a subtle ray in order that it may not be broken in the hour of your constriction upon it while you are holding it.
And it is necessary that you work exactly with scarification in the circuit of all parts of the gum and guard your strength so as not to break it and remain parted from him, and return to the patient for the malice that is greater than the first pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their foolishness and audacity on the extraction of theirs, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they bring upon men great maladies which are more difficult to be broken, and their remedies rendered useless, or some of those, or that you extract it with some bones of the mandible just as many times we have testified.
And immediately after the extraction is done, wash the mouth with hot water or warm oil and salt.
And if there is bleeding afterwards, just as many times it has happened, then immediately apply some quantity of egg white and sprinkle it over the wound, and if that egg white does not congeal, cauterize.
The form of the forceps and fine handles with which the tooth is mauled at the beginning should be of long extremity, short manubrium, and thick, in order that they may not bend when you are working with them.
And be like times of thick manubrium so that when you strain with those forceps they will not bend, with short extremities.
And be made of Indian iron with descending shapes like those of the right extremities, and in the extremities of those forceps be teeth that fit into each other and strain by strong constriction, and sometimes those extremities are shaped like the beak of a crane called ciconia, so it is guarded, and are of strong constriction.
Thirty-First Chapter.
On the Extraction of Teeth and Removal of Bones from Mandibles Gnawed.
When you must extract a tooth which is gnawed by some cause, then it is necessary that you pause over the place with a border for one day or two until the place is mollified; and immediately thereafter apply gentle or hold the extremities of those which are assembled to the beak of a crane called ciconia or guard, the form of the forceps:
Their extremities are made behind like lime or alfarfa.
And if it does not obey to your touch with these forceps or handles, then it is necessary that you dig around the root and uncover all the flesh and insert the instrument which has no name, but is a small lever whose shape is this:
And it should be of short extremity, thick and small, and not bent in such a way that it will not gnaw.
Therefore, if the root is forgotten, well it is; and otherwise, help yourself with these other instruments whose shapes are as follows: the shape of the first instrument is a triangular extremity in which there is some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it should be triangular and have a more subtle extremity.
And already you help yourself with this instrument having two ramps, the shape of which is as follows: and with other instruments which precede in the extraction of teeth; for immediately it is made with this instrument which is assembled like a great once, and this is the shape:
of a twisted or short triangular extremity with some thickness, and not bent in such a way that it will not gnaw.
And know that many of the instruments for teeth are similar to each other and resemble the other instruments.
But the artisans skilled and subtle in their art, find for themselves according to what each operation and diverse diseases mean; for they are of diseases to which the ancients did not give nor pause a proper instrument, due to the forms of various species.
But if the bone of the mandible or of the other bones of the mouth is gnawed, or if it is puffed up, then immediately be very careful about that in its place where it is convenient for the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of the roots of teeth, and help yourself with gestures like this:
And it should be in the place some thickness, little by little, and be wrapped or held in the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone of all is forgotten; and immediately restore the place with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small puffiness in the bone, then immediately remove that from that puffiness and darkness, and cure it until it is healed.
Thirty-Second Chapter.
On the Cure of Teeth Elevated above Others.
When the teeth are elevated and do not follow their natural form by those, and most especially when they appear in women, and most especially in venerable old ladies; therefore it is necessary that you consider: if a tooth is nothing like another tooth, and it is not possible to suture or bind them together, then immediately extract that one; but if it is annexed to another tooth, then you must incise it with this instrument whose shape is as follows; and it resembles a pig's mouth, and is made of Indian iron with an extremely sharp extremity.
And be your incision in which you incise it for many days due to the hardness of the tooth, and that it is not confounded with another tooth.
And if the elevated tooth is exposed to wear and tear, then immediately grind it with a file of Indian iron whose shape is as follows:
And be all of Indian iron and the edge slightly worn; and be of many small points like a file with which are made needles, with which the tooth is ground gradually over many days with ease, in order that it may not be confounded with another tooth and decay. And immediately finish grinding it to the end, and remove it with some rasors.
And if it is a tooth of which one part is gnawed and the tongue lies against it, then it is necessary that you grind it down until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and made smooth, and be filled so that the tongue does not lie against it nor corrupt the word.
Thirty-Third Chapter.
On the Restoration of Damaged Teeth with Gold or Silver Filling.
When accidents occur to the teeth in front of concussion and removal through percussion or falling, and the patient cannot strengthen over what is gnawed, and you have treated it with medicinal potions, and your treatment has not been effective, then immediately for this reason, so that they do not decay, make a filling of gold and not silver, because silver becomes green and immediately puffs up within some days, but gold is never changed.
And the filling should be in such subtlety and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the remedy is that you take the filling and double it inside two healthy teeth, and immediately increase the two curvatures of the filling between two damaged ones, or one or many, until the filling reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And immediately iterate the filling until you reach the part where you began, and strengthen your hand with ease and wisdom so that it is not damaged.
And be your constriction in which you bind the filling to the roots of the teeth so that they do not escape.
And immediately incise the superfluous extremities of the filling with forceps or toothed instruments, and add them to each place by gesture, and convert them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that it does not cause injury to the tongue. And immediately release it as straight as possible.
But if it is broken or crumbled, strengthen that one or those with another filling in such a way for all its time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the restoration of two healthy teeth and two damaged ones, tied together as these are bound.
And when one tooth or two returns to its place and settles in its own location, and they are restored as we have recommended, and remain.
And only good craftsmen and wise ones can do this.
And sometimes it is suitable for some cause of cow's bones, and it is made from them as a form of teeth, and it is placed in the location where the tooth falls, and it is strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves that purpose for a long time.
Thirty-Fourth Chapter.
On the Incision of the Ligament Which Appears in the Tongue and Prohibits Speech.
This ligament which appears behind the tongue sometimes or is natural to it, or accidental and occurs by wound that has already consolidated.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient, and raise his tongue, and immediately incise that ligament nerve according to its latitude until the tongue is freed from its recent constriction.
But if in that place there is some hardness or denudation, and it is fixed by the consolidation of the wound, then you must take care to cut and divide it according to its latitude until you have severed the ligament and dissolved the knot.
And beware that there is not a fissure made in the skin of the flesh, lest you cut and divide here, causing an artery to bleed.
And immediately wash the marks of the incision with water or vinegar or with a rough water; and immediately pause, leaving the tongue loose; thus is the method which the patient should leave his tongue in each night so that it may not be consolidated again.
But if bleeding occurs, pause over the spot with a powdered vermilion; but if the blood flows, then immediately cauterize the spot with a caustic lance which is suitable for this. And immediately apply that cure to other causes so that it may be healed.
Thirty-Fifth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Ranula Under the Tongue.
Sometimes an aposteme forms under the tongue, resembling a small tumor, which prohibits the tongue from its natural operation; and very little is magnificent enough to fill up the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, setting it in front of the sun, and consider the aposteme.
And if you see it of a brown or black color, hard as stone, to which the patient does not have any sensation, then do not touch it because it is cancerous.
And if it is whitish and moist, then you must put the index finger into it, and incise that with a subtle spatula, and cut off all around; and if blood flows in the hour of your operation, then immediately pause above powdered vermilion, which is red ochre, until the bleeding stops.
And immediately return to your work until you have completed it in the place where you began, washing it with vinegar and salt; and cure that one with another cure suitable for this, until it is healed.
Thirty-Sixth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Apostemes from Both Ears and That Which is Nothing in the Neck Gorge and Other Apostemes.
When something develops in the neck gorge, so are the glands which are gathered together, which protrude and are named two ears, which when you cure them with what we have said in the division and do not heal, then you must consider if the aposteme is hard, of brown color, without sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color and the root is large, then do not touch that one with iron for the flow of blood; but let that one mature first, and then either you will break it off or it will break off by itself.
And if it is of white color, rough, and the root of that one is subtle, then that one must be incised.
And the operation is that you consider, before your operation, if the aposteme of that one has already settled down calmly or if it has diminished by some diminution; and then make the patient sit in front of the sun, with his head in its natural position, and open his mouth, and the servant takes hold of the tongue of that one between his most prominent fingers, holding it with an instrument of noble form:
And made of silver or iron, and be subtle like a knife.
Therefore, as you take hold of that tongue and the aposteme is shown to you, and you gaze upon it with your own eyes, then take an index finger and place that one in the angle, and pull it out towards the outer parts as much as you can. Or another cause that pulls out some part of the tunics, and then immediately incise it with an instrument whose shape is this:
Or another cause that pulls out some part of the tunics, and then immediately incise it with an instrument whose shape is this: and is assembled to forceps, but the extremities of it are curved, so that the orifice of each of those is in the inward part of the other, very sharp, which is made of Indian iron well bent.
But if this instrument is not with you, then immediately incise that one with a spatula whose shape is this: And be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one notch, then incise the other one according to this manner of incision, and immediately wash the patient's mouth with rough water.
But if there is bleeding, wash with water of decoction of mulberry bark or myrtle leaves and similar to those of the stiptic causes until the bleeding is stopped, and then cure that one so that it may be healed.
And sometimes there are other apostemes in the neck gorge, besides those two ears, and incise them according to what I said earlier in the incision of two notches similarly.
And I once cured a woman of an aposteme which was born in her neck gorge, which aposteme tended towards brown color, without sensation, which had already closed the neck gorge of hers, and the woman was breathing with great stricture, and had already been prohibited from eating and drinking water, and had already come to death's door if I hadn't acted within a day or two.
And the aposteme had grown so much that it had formed two ridges, almost ready to burst through two canals.
Therefore I took hold of the instrument and stuck it in one of them, and pulled that one out, and with it came a great piece, and immediately I incised the other notch of understanding from the other canal, and immediately I opened the patient's mouth, and comprehended her tongue, and immediately I placed the instrument in that aposteme.
And immediately you cut off a great part of it, and there was only a little blood flowing from it. Therefore the neck gorge of that woman was completely opened, and she began to drink water immediately, and immediately you prescribed food. And I did not stop incising that aposteme many times for a long time, and a successive aposteme grew in its place, which I incised until it was prolonged.
Therefore I went away and cauterized the neck gorge of her, then added nothing more; and immediately I went on my way, not knowing what God had done to me.
Thirty-Seventh Chapter.
On the Incision of Hives When They are Apostemed.
When the catarrh settles on the hives, and they are apostemed and long, then it is called perpendicular. And if it is in the part behind large and rounded, and then it is called a boil.
But when it is cured with the water that we have said in the division, and your treatment does not take hold, and the aposteme itself remains, and is subtle, then you must incise that which is aggregated round; and if it reaches that length, and is bloody, of brown color or black, and there is no sensation to it, then you must be afraid of incising it, because in that case there is fear for the patient.
And when you incise it according to the manner that I have said, with regard to its whiteness and length, then make the patient sit and take hold of her tongue with an instrument whose shape is this: And immediately place the index finger in the nose, and pull that one down and incise it with one of the two instruments which are said to be for cutting notches.
And it is necessary that you do not incise it too much, but only up to the natural term without addition, because if you incise too much, you will deprive your patient of speech.
And immediately after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of two notches, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, it is necessary to use force in the cauterization of it without fear, and the manner of cauterization is only with sharp water, and you must pause the patient's head in its natural position, and immediately press her tongue with the instrument which we have said, and take from the water which we have said in the chapter on cauterization, and pour that one into it to make it live, and fill the concavity of the instrument whose shape is this:
And the extremity of it should be in the concavity where the medicine is placed, just like the concavity of a spoon, and pause the instrument with the medicine on the hives, and the patient should lie on her side for so that the saliva or mucus of the mouth flows into the intrinsically medicated area, so that no other cause falls onto the neck gorge of hers and that it is cured.
And immediately take the medicine and express it onto the hives for the space of about an hour until you see that one to be darkened and calmed by the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish, take a cloth and wrap it around the extremity of the ray, and submerge the cloth in the medicine, and insert the ray with the cloth into a canal above until the cloth adheres to that canal, and do this many times until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the hives, and then let it be so that it will dry up and fall off within three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and immediately after cauterization, wash off in the circuit of the hives with a cloth wrapped around the ray, and clean out that one with what is in the circuit of the medicine, and be washed with rough water, and be cured outside with embrocation, and inside with gentle washing until it is healed.
And this kind of cure is more safe than incision and longer in its duration.
And sometimes the hives are cured with something even more subtle than cauterization and incision, because they are cured by subfumigation in this manner: take calament, chamomile, origanum, rue, Arabic aloes, camomile, and similar herbs to those of the plants, and combine all or some of them into a bath, and let them be submerged in vinegar, and boil; and the bath should be well stirred with a decent stirrer, and there should be a canal in the middle of the bath, above which is placed an instrument made of iron, shaped like this: made of silver or iron.
And the extremity should be inserted into the patient's mouth so that the steam can reach the hives on the tongue, until the hives are somewhat obscured.
And immediately repeat this procedure many times until it is dry, and be careful not to do this cure at the beginning of the development of apostemes, because too much adjustment would occur in the aposteme, and you must not do that, but rather declare the aposteme of that one beforehand.
But if you don't have this instrument, then take a reed and tie it at the end of the tube to form a bundle of rushes, so that the patient's mouth is closed, because the closing of the rush prevents the steam from burning the mouth.
And this cure is good for health.
Thirty-Eighth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Thorns and of what adheres in the Neck Gorge and other causes.
Many times things stick together in the neck gorge or thorn from a plague or some other cause.
Then it is necessary that you extract what appears from those things on which the eye looks, because you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that it may be declared to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear and is hidden in the neck gorge, it is necessary that you make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, because then the annexed cause of the neck gorge will be forgotten through vomiting; or that you give the patient a piece of bread or a root of lattuga or a bone of dried beef; or take a piece of dry sea sponge and tie it tightly with thread, and then have the patient swallow that one, so that when the thorn reaches its place in the neck gorge where it is extracted, then it will be forgotten. And take a snake and tie it tightly with thread, and then place it behind and suddenly extract it; or take a pot and suddenly put something in it and then immediately pull it out.
But if what does not appear and is forgotten with those causes which we have said, then take one instrument of lead shaped like this:
And it should be thicker than a ray, but smaller, and at the end of it there should be a concavity which you insert into the patient's throat with ease.
And it should reach the top of the patient's head and be careful not to touch the bone of the throat, because then it will enter into the body and with that which fills up the bone or the thorn, or that one puts its hand on it.
But in truth the patient that one inserts is sweet, by reason of its knowledge of the place where the annexed cause lies and fills it up, and pulls out its hand with the instrument on top, all according to the quantity which is prepared beforehand until it is forgotten.
Thirty-Ninth Chapter.
On the Extraction of Bloodstained Matter attached to the Neck Gorge.
When the bloodstained matter is medicated with what we have said in the division of cure by medicines and it does not take hold, then consider immediately the neck gorge of the patient at sunrise, because you will have compressed the tongue of him with an instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you find the bloodstained matter, extract it with a small instrument or subtle gestures decently.
And if it is not possible to extract it with that, then take a concave tube and insert it into the neck gorge of the patient, and immediately pause in the concavity of the tube an iron instrument heated, and do this many times (until the bloodstained matter is dead and falls out of the neck gorge) and abstain from giving the patient water all day.
And immediately take a full vial of rough water, and open the patient's mouth with that, and wash the mouth with that; and do not let any cause pass through it and spit out the water an hour after another by its own hand, because the bloodstained matter will soon get angry when it tastes the water.
But if it is not forgotten with what we have recommended, then subfumigate the patient with cypress or asafoetida using the instrument which we have recommended for subfumigation of the hives.
Do this, and it will fall off in due time.
And there is another manner of operating in the subfumigation of that, which is: take a bowl in which are burning coals and cover the bowl with a cloth in the middle of which is a canal, and shape the end of the instrument in that canal; and then heat the subfumigation above; and have the patient put his mouth into the extremity of the tube, and close his mouth so that the subfumigation is not forgotten until you know that the subfumigation has reached the bloodstained matter, for the bloodstained matter will soon fall off.
And if it doesn't work, then you repeat the subfumigation many times, and abstain from giving the patient water, and make them eat salty and bitter causes, and not drink water, and thus it is necessary that this regimen be forgotten.
And this is the form of the instrument with which bloodstained matter is extracted from the neck gorge when it appears; and it resembles forceps like those, but except that there is some curvature in it. which is placed in the neck gorge, and the end of it resembles the beak of a heron, which is worn down with lime, so that when you compress any cause, it does not slip away.
Fortieth Chapter.
In which is the summary of the sermon in warning against apostemes and in dealing with them.
The apostemes are many species, of many manners, according to the memory of them, each one in this book.
And so diverse in piercing and cutting of them through two manners, of which one is by the species of apostemes in itself; and because they compress the humors.
And the second manner is proper to the places where accidents of the body occur, because the aposteme that occurs in the head is another from the aposteme that occurs in the backside; and the aposteme that occurs in the fleshy place is another from the aposteme that occurs at the joint; and each one has its own judgment by operation.
And some of the apostemes which should not be pierced, but rather allowed to mature in those places or complete their course.
So other apostemes which should be pierced when they are crossed, and not mature according to their completion, just as those that are proper to joints, for the aposteme when it comes to a joint is prolonged into what is in the circuit of it, and soon corrupts the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing a contraction of that member; or it is an apostema proper to the principal member, and if you delay the piercing of it until it becomes mature on the principal member; or it is proper to the backside, then pierce it unripe; and do not perforate it too deeply by non-completed digestion, but only that which is proper to the backside, so that the pressure of it is relieved and is elongated towards the outer parts of the backside, and a fistula is formed, or it is done in such a way that it is not healed. But it is necessary to know the hour of piercing the aposteme, and thus it is when it has matured.
And know that it has matured when there is the cessation of pain and the ceasing of fever, and immediately after, the diminution of rigor and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the aposteme, and other signs.
And when you should know that the piercing should take place in the most distant part of the aposteme and nearest to the cure of the prominence.
And piercing should be done through the length of the flesh or the body, if the apostemes are in the parts of the muscles or the fats, and in places of the ligaments, of the nerves, of the arteries; and finally, in all equal places where there is no reflection; because in those that are reflected, use piercing according to that member.
But when the aposteme is in a fleshy place, then it is better to wait until the perforation is confirmed by the digestion of that place according to its completion, as we have said; because if you pierce that one too soon, the body of the patient will be prolonged in suffering and much sorrow, and soon the lips of the patient will become hardened and the depth of it.
But some apostemes are pierced according to the latitude of the flesh or the body, as necessity demands, or according to that which the member needs.
And it is necessary to administer small piercing, and in large apostemes, great piercing and wide or many sections according to the diversity of the size of the apostemes.
And some are those of the apostemes in which it is necessary to curve the code and make an incision, when it is made so that it remains in a term that has already stopped, just as those that occur in many cases of boils and pimples.
And of those which are incised with a section having three angles; and of those which is incised like the figure of myrtle leaves, just as those of the apostemes of the hernia.
And of those which are administered with a round section and a lunar section, and resemble those.
But that to which there is no such thing as are apostemes of the lion's superficial ones, it is necessary that you pierce it with a superficial or flat piercing only.
And it is necessary when the aposteme is large and has gathered much pus, that you pierce it and do not worry about extracting all the pus at once; having extracted one part, and then sealing the aposteme with another day's passage, and then extracting a larger part, and then making many observations according to graduation until it is forgotten.
And most especially if the patient is of frail virtue, or a woman in childbed, or a child, or an old person in decrepitude, for the animal spirit many times is resolved with the exit of pus, and soon the patient dies, and you should operate on them.
Therefore, guard this chapter with great caution, and accordingly administer your piercing in which you pierce these apostemes, so that you wash away the plagues and consider then whether the plague of the aposteme is small or a flat section; if it is a small one, then you should administer some of the old remedy or poultice; and if the aposteme is great, and the fissures of the piercing are many, then it is necessary that you pause in each hole until they all come together.
And if in the aposteme is abscessed a part of the code or has been concaved in the circuit, then it is necessary that you fill it with old cloth or with a thread made from linen cloth, just as when you have torn off cloths or rags, their moisture; and strengthen that for three days; and then proceed to process it and cure the same part with what is needed from the instruments until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during the time of your operation, then you should administer rough water and vinegar in which a piece of linen cloth is steeped, and pause that over the place of the bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer remedies and treatments and poultices which I have recommended in many places of this our book and division.
But if it is in the time of winter, and the location of the aposteme is a place of many nerves, then it is necessary that you fill poultices with wine and oil warmed up, and pause those over the places.
And if it is in the summer, and the places are fleshy, then it is necessary that you pause poultices soaked in water and oil or wine and oil, and all of this be warm until the third day, just as we have said, and then it is necessary that you loosen the aposteme and wash away that one and administer to its cure what is needed for its healing.
And thus is what is necessary for the knowledge of the universal cure of apostemes, but according to the way of distinction which I have said for each and every aposteme in a particular manner, by chapters specifically.
Fortieth Chapter.
Of the section of apostemes which come in the code of the head.
In the code of the head, small apostemes, and so many species of knots that contain tunics which are at their facts; and the species of theirs are many.
And among them are pustules, and among those are ones that contain moisture similar to lime. And among them are those that contain moisture similar to dust, and resembling their own kind; and among those are ones that are lapidean, hard.
And in the section of all, and in the extraction of those there is no fear, just as you should extract them without causing contrariety to the arteries.
And the operation in section of those is that you probe them with an instrument whose form approaches thus, which is called entromes, until you know what contains.
Therefore, if what is contained is moisture, then you should incise it with a section according to the length by a superficial section in this form:
And begins from the section of the line of .B. to the line of .G. Then as the moisture is evacuated, then you should excoriate that part which contained that moisture, and do not leave any cause of it, for many times it returns, if there remains some cause.
And accordingly, submerge the cloth in Egyptian ointment, if it is present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill it with that which fills the wound and leave it for another day, because by this corrode all that remains of that skin.
And accordingly, repeat the process on that cloth with Egyptian ointment secondly and thirdly if it is needed for its healing, until you are confident that there remains no cause of that skin; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the aposteme contains fatty knots, then you should incise it in this form: with a section having the form of a cross, and cut once between two lips of the section and scrape off all that part, and exert your virtue in extracting the tunic which contains that.
But if your arteries make opposition, then do what I have recounted first of all.
And similarly do in the aposteme that is hardened by section and cure according to what we have said; and section on a hardened aposteme is even easier, because it has little blood and little moisture.
And some times there are certain of these apostemes which are in the head of some men with whom there is no moisture; and this cause is because you have incised upon an aposteme in the head of an old woman and then hardened that aposteme as a deaf, hard, white stone which could not be broken, and if any one should hit it with that, it would hurt.
But in other apostemes that occur on the head of children and ears, you should not follow this chapter, but rather follow the section for those who have had their ears pierced, but the section for all these is a superficial one.
And pause the piercing of those from the lower part of them, so that it may be relieved to the body of the pus in the lower parts.
And accordingly, cure those with what is needed for its healing.
Forty-second Chapter.
Of the section of scrofulas which occur in the neck.
Many times these apostemes are found in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those which occur in the neck, and many that are generated between them, and all of this scrofula is within its own proper tunic, and just as in the nodes and apostemes of the head, just as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for some are lapidean, and some contain moisture, and some are malignant which do not respond to cure.
Therefore, in that one which you see of good disposition in the Tocament, the color of which is manifest is propitious to the color of the code, and it is spread over all parts, and is not annexed to the nerve of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the arteries, and it is not swollen, then you should incise that one with a superficial cut from the upper part to the lower part of the body, along the line of .G. to the line of .B., and scrape off all that part, and extend two lips of the code with once, or with many times if you need those, just as we have said in the apostemes of the head, and extract it little by little.
And be more cautious not to incise the vein or nerve.
And let the spatula not be too sharp, so that it does not adjust to your hand and incision, or worry the patient, and do not incise what is not necessary to incise; for if you incise a vein or artery and prohibit yourself from performing the operation, then pause in the wound, and apply a poultice of vinegar or other powders that stop the flow of blood, and strengthen the wound, and leave it until the acute phase of the aposteme is quieted and the wound is softened and begins to be purged. When the blood is abscessed.
Therefore, return to your operation until you have completed it.
And accordingly, inquire into what your lord has said indicate if there remains any cause of the other small scrofulas; therefore, incise those and cleanse those.
And if it is in the root of the scrofula a great vein, then it is necessary that you do not incise that scrofula from its roots. Then it is necessary to tie that one with a doubled thread and let that one fall until it drops without causing any harm.
And accordingly, fill the wound with old cloth which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then cure that one.
And if you incise that scrofula entirely, then it is necessary that you adjust the two lips of the wound and sew them together as soon as possible so that there remains no superfluous portion.
But if you find that there remains a superfluity due to the greatness of the scrofula, then it is necessary that you understand how to incise its root, and excise it and administer a suture and that which is said.
And that of the scrofulas which contain moisture, perforate it with a superficial perforation where you see the location of its maturation, and pause the perforation at the point where the pus is lowest in the body, just as we have said.
And accordingly, administer a perforation, with poultices and remedies similar to those, in order that it may corrode what remains of the corruption. And accordingly, when you see that the wound is already cleansed, then you should cure it with ointment of flesh-generating substance until it is healed.
Forty-third Chapter.
Of the section of the pilhot of the aposteme which occurs in the groin area.
The ancients called this section in the pilhot, and none have seen it done by anyone in our land that way.
And this is the inscription of the sermon of those.
But in men who have scrofula, it is necessary to avoid the extraction of the pilhot, because through this, there will be no help for the disease, since all the veins and main vessels of the malady are here.
But in those who have apostemes cutaneous on the mouth or groin or both ears, when the disease is not in the skin, it is necessary to use the section of pilhot to escape the destruction which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want this, it is necessary to incise the pilhot below three circles of the pilhot or four with a small section by latitude in that which is between two circles as much as possible, so that the section is on the cartilage, not on the bone; and this location is convenient for the section, because it is devoid of flesh and the vessels of blood are there.
And if the doctor is hesitant, it is necessary that he extend the fold of the groin with once, and accordingly, make an incision in the fold until it reaches the canal, but avoid the vessels of blood if you see any cause.
And accordingly, incise the ciphac according to what we have recounted; and it is signified on the section of the canal by the flame which is of that essence and with water that is dissolved and by the absorption of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the hour is redeemed in which perforation is feared, add two lips of the fold of the wound, and sew that alone to the cartilage.
And accordingly, administer medicines that make the flesh grow until it is healed.
And I, Albucasis, say that the summary of this sermon which we have recounted is nothing but to see that some of these apostemes have already filled the groin area of the malady, and the malady has come to death, and the air in it is dried up, and some go to incise the epigloss to make the malady breathe over the wound by some respiration, and thus be saved from death.
And therefore I command to leave the wound open until the maliciousness of the disease is consumed; and it is the maliciousness of it for three days; and then I command thus: sew up the wound, and cure that one until it is healed.
And that which I have seen through my art, is that a servant took a cote and placed it in his groin area and incised with it a part of the lung's canal, and you were called to its cure, and approached the patient as if the dying were crying out, and uncovered the wound and approached the blood which was issuing from the wound slightly.
Therefore you were confessed that it had not been incised to the vein nor guided, and the breath was coming from the wound, and cured it until it was healed.
And he did not die, but rather returned to better condition in his health within a few days.
And now I say that there is no fear in the section of the pilhot.
Forty-fourth Chapter.
Of the section on an aposteme which occurs in the groin area outside, and is called a hernia of the groin.
This aposteme is known as a hernia of the groin is a great aposteme according to the color of the body, and it is more common in women.
And it is according to two manners, for either it is natural or accidental.
And in the natural one there is no need to cure.
And the accidental one is according to two manners, of which one is similar to grassy nodes, and the other manner is similar to an aposteme made by a node of an artery, and in that there is fear. And it is not necessary to make a conjecture about it except in one that is small. And you should proceed with that and investigate it according to the said, and if you find it similar to grassy nodes, and it is not suspended from any of the veins, then you should incise it as with the nodes, and extract it with what contains it of the skin and bursa; and otherwise, then examine all his being, and accordingly cure the place where healing is needed until it is healed.
Forty-fifth Chapter.
Of the section on the species of nodes.
Of the nodes, many species and I have already spoken about all their species in the division.
And it is necessary that you recount thus the difference between a node and an exitura, so that there may be no doubt.
Therefore I say that in an exitura there is heat and fever and violent pains until the ebullition of the superfluity is abated; and it is completed putrefaction which then ceases the fever, pain, and heat.
But in a node there is no heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that ciphac lion which is at the proper sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a cheese and it is made thus as if a vacuum, whether greater or lesser, and they are according to two species, either fatty and containing moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, as you have said in the division.
Therefore, when you come to the cure of a node, it is necessary that you expose it and inquire about it at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intro-mes, according to its form as will be described in the chapter that is next.
And this is the manner of inquiring about apostemes and all nodes, which is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most humid part which you find in the apostema, and you rotate those fingers in that way little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated to the core.
And accordingly, lastly, your hand according to the measure of the greatness of the aposteme, and accordingly, extract the intro-mes and consider what is found in its wake.
And if the fluid that has been extracted is forgotten and runs off, regardless of its color, then you should incise it with a superficial section according to what I have said in other apostemes.
And if from the wake of the intro-mes there is no fluid, then you know that it is fat.
And cut above it with an incision having the form of a cross according to this form: in this way as I have taught you, and suspend it with once, and excise the core of all parts with ease, and preserve the skin or sac, if possible, over that and extract that pus from the node.
But if it is broken in the operation, then it is not possible to extract that pus, and many times you will have to put it back together, and accordingly, you should extract it bit by bit until there is no longer any cause from it.
Therefore, if there is no longer any cause from it, little or much, the node will return to its original state.
And if you find that there remains a small part from it, then complete the wound with some sharp and corrosive powders and strengthen the wound, and pause over that which contains the aposteme cautiously, and cure that one from another cure until it is healed.
And if it is a great node, then you should sew up the two lips of it and cure that one with what engenders.
But if the pulsating vein is on the opposite side, or non-pulsating, then you should press and humbly the place of the scar that is dry and let it rest for two days or three until the wound is purged and the ebullition of blood has ceased; and accordingly return to incising what remains of the node.

Forty-seventh Chapter.
Of the cure of men's breasts which are similar to women's.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become swollen as they start to be relaxed, and accordingly, the breasts are swollen, and similar to those of women, and aposteme-like, distant.
And for him who has this condition, you should incise those with a lunar section according to this form, from the line of B to the line of G, and accordingly, excise all the excess skin, and accordingly, fill the wound with an encarnative medicine, and accordingly, adjust the two lips of the wound with stitching, and cure that one until it is healed.
But if the breast declines to its natural shape and is softened by its magnitude just like in women, then it should be divided into two parts as far as possible, with two sections resembling a lunar figure, of which each one is contained within the other until the end of each one, so that the major line contains the minor one according to this form from the line of B to the line of G. And accordingly, excise the core between the two lips of the wound, and remove the swelling, and administer what we have said about suturing and powders which are necessary for the cure until it is healed.
And if you do not complete the incision of what needs to be cut due to the unease of the patient and the flow of blood, then you should fill the wound with a cloth submerged in corrosive ointment and leave it until the corrosion that remains from the grayish color is removed, and accordingly, cure that one until it is healed.
Forty-eighth Chapter.
Of the perforation of apostemes which come after the titillation.
Those apostemes that come after the titillation are of the species of scrophulas, hard ones, which contain a skin that is drawn out.
And among those are some that contain moisture; and those that contain moisture should be incised with a lunar section according to this figure: from the line of G to the line of B. And when all the fluid that is in the apostema has been evacuated, then you should fill it with a piece of well-greased cloth and leave it until the next day.
And accordingly, the cloth should be left in place and cured with a suitable ointment for that, until it is healed.
And if the aposteme is of the species of nodes or scrophulas, then you should divide it as we have said in the section on scrophulas, equally.
And if the wound is extended in such a way that it would be painful to burn, then you should administer in it a cautery according to what we have previously said.
Forty-ninth Chapter.
Of the section of apostemes that occur by artery or by vein.
When an artery is broken and the cord which is over it is consolidated, many times a post-hemorrhagic aposteme occurs. The signs by which it is known that inflammation and aposteme are due to an artery or vein: for when the apostema is due to an artery, it becomes long and swollen in the breast of the chest; and when you compress the apostema with those very words, you will feel a stridor.
And that which is due to a vein is rounded on the surface of the chest.
And the section on this apostema is an error, and more so in those who are left with abscesses, and in the armpits, and in the neck, and in many places of the chest; and it is very great, therefore its cure by iron should be avoided, and that which is in the extremities or at the head should be avoided.
But on that which is due to them by inflammation of the orifice of the artery, you should divide the section in the cord along its length; and accordingly, you should cut through the section with scissors and approach the artery, and deliver the tunics until it is uncovered.
And accordingly, you should tie that thread and make it pass through the other side, and tighten the artery with a double suture in two places,  which causes you to see ahead in extracting two arteries which are in the temples.

Sixty-ninth Chapter.
Of Castration.
Know that castration in our law is excommunicated, but by reason it has been granted to me to recall it in this book; and it is not necessary to recall it except for two manners, of which one is because of the science of the doctor when he is questioned about it, so that he may know the cure of it which occurs; and the other manner is that many times we have needed castration of other beasts for our uses through those animals, such as mares, oxen, and pigs, and similar to beasts.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two manners: or by attrition, or by incision by section.
But in that which is done by attrition, the way of the operation is that we make the beast lie in cold water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang. And accordingly, take those with your own hand until they are resolved and do not appear to leave any touch.
But in castration done by incision and section, it is necessary that you hold the beast and press the cord of the testicles with your left hand.

And accordingly, insert those so that you will have whatever is excoriated. And do not leave any cause on the membrane of the cord, but only the subtle membrane which is above the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by attrition, because attrition gently leaves no cause of the testicles so that the beast desires to copulate.
And accordingly, cure the wound until it is healed.

Seventy-fifth Chapter.
To teach midwives in what manner they should be medicated when the bodies are sick according to a non-natural figure.
